MIRACLES LATELY VVROVGHT BY THE INTERCESSION OF THE GLORIOUS VIRgin Marie, at
Mont-aigu, nere unto Siche in Brabant.

Gathered out of the publik instruments, and informations taken thereof. By
authority of the Lord Archbishop of Maclin.

Translated out of the French copy into English by M. Robert Chambers Priest,
and confessor of the English Religious Dames in the City of Bruxelles.



PRINTED, At Antwarp, by Arnold Conings.

1606.

Cum Priuilegio

TO THE MOST HIGH AND MIGHTY PRINCE, JAMES BY THE GRACE OF GOD KING OF GREAT
BRYTAIN^, France, and Ireland, Defender of the Faith.
I DOUBT not (Dread So^ueraine) but that by some means the report of the
strange, and wonderful things which lately have happened in these Netherlands
are come to your grace, knowledge, where at all the world bear stands so much
amazed and astonished: but whether your Majesty has been informed of at the
parti^ularities, I justly doubt, because I can not by any ways understand,
that your Highness is any whit moved Meta^li. 1. tex. 1.
therewith. Aristotle says. Omnes hommes naturaliter scire desiderant. All
men have a natural desire to attain to the true, and perfect knowledge of
things: but the wiser, and learneder sort are especialy adicted thereunto,
principaly, when the object is admirable, of weight and importance: for then as
it yields unto their spirits extraordinat^e contentment and perfection, so to
be ignorant thereof, puts them in hazard of error, no^^ble damage, and
confusion. Whence has proce^ded the exquisit diligence and curious
inquisition, which we have seen used in this present matter, not only by our
Prelates, Doctors, and Professors of learning, unto whom the direction of our
souls in the way of God does chiefly appartaine, but also by the temporal
Princes, No^^li^e, and men of civil pol^^cie, whose wisdom and due regard of
their own credites, d^sdained, to p^rmit them selves to be led by the noses, to
the future peril of their souls, together with the present disparagement of
their honors, and seputations. The which manner of these men's proceeding in
this affair, gave me occasion to suspect, that your Majesty was not
sufficiently informed hereof: for other wise, it is not to be imagined
(consideration being had of the common and general opinion which men here
conceive of your Graces singular prudence, and lite^arure) that your Royal
wisdom would pass over these things as though your grace had no apprehensi^n
or esteem thereof, which of very many are held as most per^icious, and
abominable, in the sight both of God and m^n: and of innu merable others are
reputed as most venerable, and glori^us demonst^ations of our Lord his sweet
love care and providence ^owardes his loving Spouse his Church* which he
vouchsafes to adorn in this honorable and admirable manner.

Upon which conjecture my Gracious lord and souueraine I have adventured to
send unto your Princely view the authentical ^elat on of diverse things, which
in these last years have happened amongst us: a subject undoubtedly not
unworthy your ma^^re consideration, and exquisit judgment. And I have
labored to translate it out of the Fie^ch copy, not that I thinck your
Majesty ignorant of that language, but that by the way I might also satisfy
the greedy desire of many your ma^esties loving subjects, who have alredie
hard the b^uit but yet have not had the assured and certain knowledge of the
matter. And I am the more bold to write unto your Majesty of these things,
considering what by others has bin done unto great Emperors, and kings in the
like case. For I read that Pome^^s Diverse authors who have vvr^tten to Princes
of mir^cles
Pilate wrote unto Tyberius Caes^r touching the miracles of our Lord and
Savior. I note how the famous. Nicephorus dedicated his Ecclesiastical

here unto, for that it is not unknown, what extraordinary pleasere and

that appertain unto God.

What things do more apertaine unto God (Gracious Sovereign, then miracles*
which is as much to say, as those works which can be wrought only by God, of
which rank these (where of here relation is mad^) are believed to be, as
being semblable unto those divine operations, where by the Savior of the
world did manifest h^s Godhead to the world. For here your Grace may behold,
how the blind see, the deaf hear, the lame walk, the devils are ex pelled he
leaporous are made clean, and many other painful, deformed and desperate
maladies are cured.

I know it would be a great miracle if these works would be admitted ^f all men
for miracles. Wherefore I am humbly to entreat your gracious patience, to
peruse what here I have set down in an swear of those objections, whereby I
find them ord narilie to be oppugned I fear that I shall be deemed of many to
be over prolix, but I beseech them to consider, that it is Gods cause which I
handle, and that I endeavor to inform my gracious Lord and Prince in such
sort of the verity hereof, that with greater light and assurance he might
pronounce his expected royal sentence, to the glor^e of God, his own honor,
and inestimable commodity of many thousands. And although I have not here so
convenient place to utter the hundre^h part of those things which otherwise I
wou^d therefore I intend (by our Lords h^lp) here after more to enlarge my self
herein as occasion shall b^ offe^ed*

THE TRANSLATOR TO THE CHRISTIAN Reader.
THERE is a certain foul fault (gentle Reader) for which we Catholics are very
ordinarily and odiously reproched, which is, that we are passing light and
exceedingly prone to believe every fable: and upon this blind simplicity
easily drawn into any superstition and error touching the worship of God. The
reason whereof is held to be our ignorance in the word of God, and the little
sight we have in the holy Bible: rather harkening to the traditions of men (as
is the phrase) and to the voice of the Church, then in marcking what the written
word teaches us. It is very certain that Catholics say with the Royal Ps l.
77. vvh^ ^r^ ditions are hi(...) reg^^ded by Catholics
Prophet: Q^anta mandauit patribus nostris nota facere ea filijs s^^s? vt
cognoscat generatio altera. Filij qui nasc^ntur and exurgent and narrabunt
filijs suis. How great things did God commau^d our For^fathers to m^^^ them
known to their children that an other generation may know them? The sons
that were to be borne and to rise up after them they shall tell them again to
their children. As our blessed Savior did, when Mat. 16.
sending his Apostles to instruct the world, he bad them preach, that is,
deliver by word of mouth, or tradition his word, but he commanded them not to
write his word. And so from them (upon the tradition of our Elders* we receive
what the Apostles have said or written to be Gods word, although we find no
such declaration for neither their Epistles or Gospels, in the book of his
word. Which thing we accomplish according to the commandment of Moses
saying, Interroga Patrem tuum, and annunci^ti^: tibi: maiores tuos, and dicent
D^^t. 32
tibi Ask thy sa^^er and he vvil declare unto thee: demand of thy^^ aunceters
and they vvil tell thee: which thing our Aduersairies themselves obse^ue, in
admitting upon the credit of the Catholic church the books of holy Scripture:
upon which ground follows how we ought to submitt our senses and judgments
to whatsoever the said Catholic church shall teach us: else blott that article
out of the creed: Credo Ecclesiam Catholicam I believe Math. 1^
the Catholic Church: else let those words of our blessed Savior be scraped
out of the Gospel, St Ecclesiam non aud^^rit ^is ^ibi sicut Ethnicus and
Publicanus if he vvil not hear the Church. Aug co^. 11. Epist. fundamenti.
let him be to thee as (...) Heathen and Publicane. and consequently let Saint
Augustine be hissed out of his pulpit for saying: Euangelio non trederem. (...)
me Ecclesia commoueret auctoritas. I would not b(...) the Gospel, except the

us, and without understanding our case they blyndely condemn us, and so they

verdict of God his holy word, and first I will begin with Pilgrimages.

It is a sure and certain verity which the Catholic church always
confesses, that God is in every place: for so he himself has Hierem. 23.
avouched, that he fills both heaven and earth, and therefore there is no
place where in he may not be honored and called upon. Yet, as our lord has
sanctified some particular Exodus 20
days for his service, so has he made choice of some particular places where
in he would The use o^ Pilgrimages ^ oo ^ed by holy scripture.
be particulerly honored For he commanded Abraham to offer his son Isaac
upon one certain determinate hill. Jacob had by vision that the mount Bethel
was a place of extraordinary sanctity, and for that cause he said. Quam
terribilis est locus iste? non est hic aliud nisi domus Genesis 12.
dei, and porta calls. How dread full is this place? this place is nothing else
but the hows of God, and the gate of heaven. Which asseueration of the
Patriarch, our Lord did so much approve, that he took to himself the Gem 31
title of that place saying to the said, Jacob Ego sum Deus Bethel* I am the
God of Bethel. So Hore^ was called the mountain of God, which our Lord
esteemed so holy, that he caused Moses Exodus 3.
his especial servant to put of his shoes in De^t. 12.
honor and reverence thereof. Finally our Lord made choice of one particul^r
place, in which only, he would have his people to offer 3 Reg. 1
sacrifise unto him: where when Solomon had erected his Temple he bese^^hed God
not only to bestow certain particular preuileges and miraculous favors upon
such as prayed there in, and upon such strangers as came from far Cuntries in
pilgrimage thither, but upon all such as should in their prayers turn their
faces toward the holy place, when upon other just impediments they could not
perform their devotions in the holy Temple it self. Which manner of
worshipping God (by pilgrimages) ended not after wards, for that our Savior
foretold the Samaritane that: Ver^es hora quando neque in monte hoc, neque in

The reason of which doctrine (if thou mark The ground and reason of al these
points.
well courteous reader) consists in these poincts? First, to worship and honor
God for himself, as being the Fountain of all goodness: then his saints, for
that he has imparted and derived unto them the abundance of his unspeakable
graces and glory: and so the images of them both are to be worshipped as their
representations, and for that they notably apertaine unto them: and thus
consequently Catholics do very well if for the honor of God and of his Saints,
they discreetely and orderly love, and reverence any thing that concerns
them. Which honor and reverence as they may profess with their mouths, or by
their words, so may they manifest the same accordingly and in the same degree,
by their outward gestures and actions. For which cause they are much to be
blamed who blame the devotions of Catholics, for honoring the Relics of
Saints, or such things as belong unto them, or to their very images. As we see
the good devout Catholics here, for the honor they bear to God, to worship
his worthy Mother and for her worship, they reverence her Image: and for the
reverence of her Image, they bear also a reverent esteem of the very wood of
the tree wherein the said Image was placed. Acording to that which is recorded
in Exodus: where the ^xod. ^.

dead corpse of Elize^s, the 4 Reg. ^ 4 Reg 1^ Act 5. Act. 1^.

THE PREFACE OF THE AVTHOR.
We give thee here (loving Reader) a brief rebearsal of such things as
concern the place of Montaigue nere ^o the town of Siche^ in the Duchy of
Braba^t: We have also set down the miracles which have ^apned there of late,
by the merits and inter^ession of the glorious virgin Marie, Yet not all, but
only those that are proved and veri^^ed by pu^like wrytings and attestations
of Magistrates, and other authentical declarations: as well to prevent thereby
the slanders of h^ret^kes, as also for the better information and satisfaction
of good Catholics. As for the he e^^kes, they have all ^ayes had an ancient
custom with great vehemencie^ to calu^^^ate and blame those things in the
Catholic Roman Church, which they in their synagogs li^t not to believe. And
before the^ the Pharisees used the like, who ascribed the miracles of our
Savior and his disciples to the power of the Devil. This all sectaries that
have been since the beginning in the Church have always imitated, and the
beret^kes of our days do still very markablie practice, who began their
heresies by uttering blasphemses against the Moother of God and his Satnctes.
And pullskly not only in their talk, but also in their writings, bragging
themselves to be Image-breakers.

A certain pamphlet in flemmish came of late to our hands printed and
composed (as it seems) by seem pernicious Caluinist of Holland against the
honor done to the Moother of God at Mountaigue. In which pamphlet he writes
execrable blasphemies, he forgeth fables and lies against those of the Church,
he basely scosseth at the pi^tie of Princes and Lords of the Country, and at
all such as love and serve the glorious virgin And sinallie he endeuereth by
lies to deface and obscure the honor done to God and his blessed Mother.
Howbeit this companion and all such like night-o^les will they will they, must
needs endure the most clear light which e^r lord makes daily to ^lase before
our eyes, to his own and his Moothers glory: and through the evidence of
these miracles, they must ly trodden under the feet of this woman, together
with the old serpent their head and captain.

And in vain shall their labor be that endeavor to hinder the Sun to give
his light unto the earth, the will of our Lord being such as the truth shall
clerelie be seen, and pterce through the most obscure and duskie clouds of
heresies, Far more proffitable therefore and wholesome would it be for them to
open the windows of their hearts and to let these divine beams enter in. And
what beams can be more clear or of more force, then these so palpable
arguments and evident demonstrations of the pure and sincere verity. Do not
these (I beseech you) so apparent miracles cry out the very same that our
Savior speaks in the ghospel? If you believe not me, believe the woorks
which I do. And that which the Parents of the blind man said. Enquire of him,
he is of age, he will answer for him-self, he is thirty years old.

These corrupters of the truth think to abuse the simple people, telling them
that Catholics woorship Idols, and that they honor the virgin Marie as if she
were a goddess, and adore Images: but this is an old song so often by them
chaunted, so often disputed, and in such sort refuted as there is no Catholic
of so sie^der indgemen^ who knows not the blessed virgin to be honored as a
Patroness and Advocate to her Son and not as a Goddess, and that the honor
done to her Image has relation to her person and not to ber Image. And
m^r^o^er that all manner of adoration whether it be Latria, Dulia, or
Hyperdulia must not be taken or understood according to the outward woork and
appearance but according to the intention of the party from whom it proceeds.

After an other sort this may in like manner be proffitable to Catholics. for
so much as there are some amongst them who seem to doubt of these miracles, for
diuersmen by a certain kind of incredulitte, and others upon a spirit of
contradiction either deny that these are miracles, or they will not believe,
that they have verily been wrought. Unto these we must say that which our
Savior said unto Saint Thomas. Come and feel etc. If they will not yield
credit to that which we set down, let them go and see, and feel with their
fingers, or else enquire the verity of those who have been cured, for I am
assured they shall find good store of them. And if after this they will not yet
believe, assure your selves (as Aristotle said of those that denied the
principles of sciences) that they do it for want of wit and judgment, or else
it must be beaten into their brains with beetles or bastinadoes.

There are also others who take occasion to doubt hereof, because many having
been miraculously cured, did not for all that obtain their health at an
instant, or at the very place, but by little and little with some alteration of
their bodies: unto whom we may by good reason answer, that to prove a
miracle it suffiseth that the operation and effect be above the course of
nature, and not by any natural means or remedies: and such are all those
miracles which here in this history we have set down.

Moreover amongst these miracles some are more perfect and notable then some
others are: amongst the more notable are esteemed those, when the diseases are
cured at the very same instant, whereof we have very many, and yet the
others are not without miracle, although they have happened in success of time,
because they were wrought above the course of nature.

For who is he that will or can deny that when our Lord cured the blind man it
was a true and perfect miracle? who at the beginning saw men walk like trees,
yet no man doubts that he afterward received his perfect sight.

Neither is it any ways repugnant to the nature of a miracle that at the time
of their healing they feel a change or alteration either in their souls or in
their bodies: for (not alleging the other reasons which we will set down in
the ensuing history) who is he that knows of those whom our Lord and his
saints miraculously cured did not feel the like also in them selves? No author
nor writer denies or affirms that they were restored to their health without
such alterations. And for so much as those miracles were wrought principalli^
for the confirmation of our faith, it suffi^ed only to set down the bare truth
without further expressing the circumstances, or what other things else therein
occurred. Our Lord recounted his miracles to the disciples of S. John Baptist
(who doubted whether he were the true Messiah or no) without making any mention
of the circumstance* or other qualities, saying: Go, tell unto John. The blind
see, the lame walk, the deaf hear, the leprous are made clean, giving us
thereby to understand that such cures (although he had said nothing) yielded
certain and infallible proof that he was the true Messiah. And when we hear,
see, and feel the like to be done by the merits and intercession of the
glorious mother of God at the Mountain of Mountaigue, who is he that is so
wilfully blind as will not acknowledge the holy Catholic Roman Church adorned
and honored with such signs and miracles, to be the only spouse of Christ, the
dove and beloved of our Lord, the fortress and pillar of truth, in whoms
only true and perfect miracles are to be found?

Awake therefore (O you misledde ^ith heresies) open the eyes of your
understanding, and behold the light wherewith the God of mercies vou^^safeth to
lighten the darkness of your iniquities and ignorances. If he had not publikly
wrought these signs and miracles that none but he is able to woork, perhaps
this misguided people might in some sort be excused: but seeing they have had
so great and manifest signs and testimonies, they shall be inexcusable in that
they do not woork their own conuersions.

And you faithful Catholics who for the prosperity of our enemies, or evil
success of the affairs of our religion, or for the abuses and imperfection in
the life and ma^ners of those of the Church or layetie, are wont to stagger or
be faint-harted, confirm and settle your selves hereby: you are in S. Peeters
ship, wherein (not withstanding all waves and tempests) it pleases our lord to
woork his miracles.

And all you that by the miracles and interces^ion of the glorious virgin Marie
most holy Moother of God, have re^eiued health or help, forbear not to
manifest the same to your superiors and others (since it is much honorable to
reveal the works of God) whom we exhort in our Lord that they give
advertisement thereof to the right Reverend the lord Archbishop of Maclin,
VVherby the name of God may be sanctified amongst us, and that his mercy may
be exalted and magnified in his Sai^cts. For how many so^u^r his miracles a^e,
so many exhortatio^s and sermons they be, and which more forciblie mo^e the
hearts of men then the sermons of any ^e ^e never so excellent for his knowledge
or elequence. And for our part let us endeavor to give everlasting praise to the
mercy of God, who in this our miserable and afflicted state (as for the
present is this of the low Countries) he vouchsafes to send us comfort by
woorking so great and goodly works, yea such as we think in that country
never happened the like.

Lt us also praise the moother of God, and let us so beg her protection as
she rising once like an amiable Aurora, will disperse the dark obscurity of
our miseries, and as a most bright and fair Moon, will vouchsafe to yield
light to those which ly in the dark night of errors and heresies, and as a
clear shyning Sun would pearce with the beams of grace and virtue the
hearts of the faithful people, which have the fear of God: and finally like a
well ^arshalled battle drive all the heresies out of the woorld: and by her
holy protection defend these low Countries from all corporal and spiritual
in^^sions: by the power of him who does her such honor both in heaven and in
earth*

Amen.
THE HISTORY OF THE MIRACLES WROVGHT BY THE INTERCESSION OF OUR blessed Lady,
at a place called Mountaigue, in the duchy of Brabant.
THE holy scripture makes mention of an Oak behynd the town of S^chem in
Palestine under the which Jacob buried the Idols and the Jewels that hanged at
the ears of his people. And at the same Oak the Captain Iosue renewed the
covenant betwixt God and the people of Israel. Very fitly ought we to set
forth the renowned fame of the Oak which stands nere to our Sic^en in the
Duchy of Brabant, at the which Oak in a place vulgarly called Scherpenheuuel
and in French Mountaigue before an Image of the Mother of God being very
simple and of little show (as the place it self is) it pleases his divine
majesty to woork daily many miracles, by the glory whereof it seems that
his desire and pleasure is that the heretics being curbed, and the Catholics
made more submissiue the pride of heretics and the idolles of other vices
might here ly buried: and that in this place he will be appeased by the prayers
of good and godly Christians: and that he will renew his friendship and
alliance with the people of these low Countries, for we esteem the glory of
miracles to be accounted one of the most assured notes and marks of the true
Church, grounded upon the words of our Savior in the gospel saying: Such
signs shall follow Marc. 16 Heb^. 2.
those that believe in me. They shall lay their hands upon the diseased and
they shall be healed, etc. And the holy Apostle says that unto the preaching
and teaching of the way of salvation, our Lord added a testimony of his own
hand by signs, miracles, and other supernatural works of his power. For even
as the miracles of our Lord served for proofs to make me^ believe that he was
the true Savior of the world according to that which he says in the gospel,
even so the true and undoubted miracles which cannot be wrought without the
puissance of God, yield testimony to his body, which is his holy Church. For
which cause that most learned De vtilit^te credendi cap. 17. eodem lib. cap. 1^
and co^ tra op. funda. cap. 4.
Doctor S. Austin says, that the Catholic Church obtained sovereign
authority, when heretics were judged and condemned by the majesty of
miracles. And in an other place, that the authority of the Church began by
miracles, and by the same she mainteyneth her force. And again, the concord
and union of all nations (says he) together with the authority of miracles,
holds me in the holy Church. And Richard de S. Victore durst co^fide^tlie
lib. 1. de T^init^te cap. 2.
say, Lord if this be erroneous which we believe, it is by thyself that we
are deceived, for these things were established and confirmed amongst us with
such signs and miracles as none but thou were able to woork.

Amongst these miracles they are not the least which his divine goodness
vouchsafed to woork even from the beginning of the Christian common wealth, in
diverse places at the Images and for the honor and woorship of the Mother of God.

It is an undoubted ancient tradition that S. Luke painted an Image of our
Lady, ^^e which we have by the testimony of diverse credible writers and
authors, as of Theodorus, Nicephorus, Metaphr^sies, But lib. 14. in vita S.
Luce.
whether he painted more then one we are not assured, yet this is certain,
that many Images were drawn according to that which he had first painted, and
were afterward sent to diverse places, neither ought it to be deemed
impertinent that some have attributed them to S. Luke, for so much as they were
perfect draughtes and resemblances of the original first painted by his own
hand. Of which Images that was one, which S. Gregory the first carried A^^^*
S^^^^: lib de (...) ^^^^ Eccle Baron in Annal.
in solemn procession in Rome, whereby the great infection of the plague
ceased, where with the air was corrupted, in the year of our lord 509. And
all the people of Rome are of opinion that it is the same Image which is now
kept in the Church of S. Maria Mayor. Such also was ^aspar var^e^ius in
cho^ogr^ Hisp.
that, which (as they say) the said S. Gregory sent to S. Leander Bishop of
S^^^l in Spain, the which the Spaniardes honor and now call by the name of

They who have left us written the history and particularities of that

THE SECOND CHAPTER.
AND to the end we occupy not our selves in foreign matters only. These low
Countries have in semblable manner (and that in many places) the memories of
the glorious Vergin Marie honored with diverse miracles: And to begin with
Henault: Tongre, a village hard by the Town of Ath has an Image of our Lady,
the which in the year 1081. was three times placed there, and notwithstanding
that it was diverse times carried to other places, yet was it still mirac^louslie
brought thither back again.

In the Abbay of Cambron (which is of the Cistertian order) is kept a certain
Image of the Mother of God, painted upon a wall, the which in the year 1^32
being wounded with a bore-speare by a Jew, yielded great abundance of blood,
the marks whereof are to be seen to this present day. We read the like to
have happened even in our days in the year 1595 in the Duchy of Sauoy nere to
a town called Mo^ntaruie, where there was an Image of our lady, which having
received certain gashes with a sword of a Caluinist, yielded incontinently
blood, wherewith many blind, deaf, lame, and other diseased were cured.

In like manner our Lord has wrought many marvelous things by the invocation
of his holy Mother in a little town of Henault called Chieure, since the year
of our lord 1326. the which also continues even to our days.

And in special sort in the same province the said blessed virgin is much
honored in the town of Hall where is an Image of her, placed by S. Elizabeth
daughter to the king of Hungary, singular devotion being there used in regard
of the miracles which are see^ there to happen unto those who either go thither
in pilgrimage or else vow to go. Whereof the great lear ned I. Lipsius has of
late composed a book.

In the Country of Art^o^s and in the City of A^ras is a wax taper brought
Joan Molan. not. S^. ^elg 6. ^ebr.
thither miraculouslie by the Mother of God in the year of our lord 1105 in
the time of Lambert Bishop of Arras. And this was done to heal a disease
called the fyri-burning. This Candle is kept with great care in the foresaid
City and is used very souerainlie for the curing of many maladies: never
consuming although Ioannes Mo^ us in pt^^: spirit.
it be often times lighted; and that they have made many little candels of the
drops of the wax that have been powered from the same: not unlike unto that
candle of John the Heremit dwelling in S^chus near to Jerusalem, the which he
had in his cave burning before an image of our lady, and which he was wont to
recomend to the Mother of God, to the end she would still keep it burning for
her honor, so often as at any time he went to any place thence in pilgrimage.
And although he was sometimes from home. for the space of two, three, yea of
six months together, yet he always found it at his return whole and clearly
burning as before he left it. Lykwise many great works and liberal gifts of
God are seen to proceed from him, in the duchy of Braba^t, by the intercession
of the glorious virgin his mother.

At Laken nere to the city of Bruxels a fair Church has been built, to the
honor of our Lady, wherein (as it is well known to the world) many have
received help and remedy from the hand of God. So as the woorthines of this
place confirms sufficiently the ancient tradition, that the first model or
pattern of the said Church was set down by our Lady herself and honored with
her visible presence.

In the City of Bruxell^s in a Chapel of our Lady call ed the Sablon an Image
Antiqu. M^S ex b^b^^^ ^^^b^aeh v^lli^.
of her was there set in the year of our Lord 1348. by a very devout woman
called B^^iue S^etkens, the which Image is thought to have been pa^nted by
miracle, and in this place many miracles were wrought until the year 1580.

In certa^ne old written book^s in the Collegial Church of S. Peter's in
Louaeine are conta^ned ^undrie miracles which have in times past happened before
an Image of our Lady which is yet there, and these have continued since the
year 1442. even unto out time.

In Viluord, in a Cloister called of co^foit where the Rel g^euse women of the
Carm^litan order dwell, is an Image of our lady the which Dame Sophia wife to
duke Henry the second of B^aba^t received from her mother S. Fliza et*
Countess of He^sen, which she left there for the comfort of certain o^d
Matrons that then ly^ed in a congregation in that place, whereupon it has still
retained that name.

It is not needful to recount here the miracles which our Lord woorketh day lie
by the invocation of his blessed mother in the Church of our lady of Hanswick
at Ma^klin, Alsembe^g and Sc^ute nere Bruxelles, at Led by the town of Alost,
at Hasselare by A^denard in Flaundres, at the Chapel of our lady in the
market place of S. Omers in Arthois, at the Chapel of our lady of grace nere
to the City of Lile, And it is thought that there is no one province in these
low Countries, which has not, or had not, some place dedicated to the Mother
of God, wherein his divine bounty has not wrought or yet woorketh not
sometimes miracles, where of there are both particular and public
testifications in great number.

THE THIRD CHAPTER.
BUT amongst all, that is very wounderful which we see and hear to happen
daily at the place of Mountaigue nere to the town of Siche^, in the Duchy of
Brabant, being of such importance and admiration that through the fame thereof
within the space of six or seaue^ mo^thes many thousands of Pilgrims from all
parts have resorted thither. And to the end that in a matter so lately known
and in such abundance of miracles amongst the brute of so-many incertainties,
the truth may perfitly be seen, we have resolved to make here a particular
and faithful rehersall of such things as hitherto have come to our knowledge,
as well concerning the p^ace and the Image as of the miracles. Which have happened,
according to the informations gathered by the authority of the right Reverend
lord the Archbishop of Mecklin, howbeit through the diversities of times and
distance of places wherein the persons dwell unto whom God has imparted his
favors, it has been impossible to come to the perfect knowledge of each thing
that has befa^ne them, which notwithstanding we hope may be discovered in
time, and come to light, whereof as yet we have no knowledge, partly through the
negligence or simplicity of such as have experimented them in them selves, and
partly for that many are (perhaps) ashamed to publish their secret accidents
and maladies, although they have been miraculously cured thereof.

THE FOVRTH CHAPTER.
BUT before we proceed any furthes it will not be impertinent (considering in
this affair the great providence and goodness of God) to examine a little the
particular circumstances of the place, of the Image, and of other things
appertaning to this matter. It is a thing much to be noted, that seeing in many
rich and mighty Cities and other places of strength in these low Countries,
there are many stately Churches dedicated to the glorious Virgin Marie, and
that she has in so many places her Images in gold and silver, and painted or
carved with great art and woorkmanship, yet notwithstanding our Lord God (who
is the greatness of the humble) and our blessed Lady the mother o^ humility,
would make choice of this rude and desert hillock, in the territory of S^c^en,
a very poor town, amongst a company of poor and ruyned people, at a small
Image made of a silly piece of wood of the height of a foot and a half or
little more, pa^nted and set forth very fim ply, placed in an old Oak, and
that upon the frontiers of those that are enemies and rebels to our Princes.

The which things seem to me full of many masteries, And ^ist that what soever
concerns the place and Image, s very base and a^i^ct. What other thing does
it signify, then that which the Prophet David says, That the ^^g^ lord
respects the things that are humble? And that which the Apostle says, Our
lord has chosen the abject t^ings of this world? And that our Lord Jesus
Christ did mean that this place which he had appointed for his own and his
mothers honor should be like to the places of their habitation in this world,
where he made choice of two very low places, to wit, the one, that of Nazareth
for his incarnation and abode, and the other that of Bet lem with the poor
cribbe, for his natiuine. How poor and miserable that quarter of Sec^en has
been, with how many mishaps and calamities it was wholly ruined and brought to
desolation, will not be impertinent to declare, for the comfort of those poor
afflicted people the inhabitants thereof.

The town of Sic^en before these troubles had a fair parish Church dedicated
to S. Eustace, well furnished with diverse altars, ornaments and goodly belles.
Moreover it had a Cloister of Religiouse women of the order of S. A^stin, with
their Church and fa^re buildings accordingly. And now this town is so ruined,
that at this present there is not so much as one bell, The Church and steeple
are for the most part vnco^ered, There are few altars, and very small store of
ornaments that have been saved out of the fire, The Cloister is all burnt
down, saving one Chapel, and one little hows which before was built for the
sick. Moreover the said town had about twelve or thirteen hundredth Burgeses,
many of them were welthy and all of competent means and ability, where at
this present it is hard to find 300. souls therein, or in the places which are
dependant of the same, and all those are so needy and low brought that there
is not one of them of that ability as to entertain his friend with a decent
lodging.

This town here-to-fore had many fair houses built with brick and now they
are all made with mudde, and thatched with straw in form of a village, which
is no wounder if you consider what the town has sustained during these
troubles and civil garboiles.

After the defait of Ge^blours which happened in the year 1578. and the 28. of
February the Town and castle being well manned with a strong garrison was
besieged by the soldiers of the Catholic king, and after battrie being
entered by force, the soldiers of the garrison, were either put to the sword,
or drowned, or hanged, many of the Burgeses slain, and the town given over to
fire and pillage. Not many years after, it was taken again, sometimes by the
rebels, sometimes by the kings people. It was six times spoiled, and once
consumed even to ashes. Besides all this it was grieuouslie afflicted with the
plague and wholly deuowred and eaten up with the great garrisons which lay
therein continually. And not only the City, but the Country round about was
brought to such desolation by the ranging up and down of soldiers thieves and
murderers, that the Country, (for some miles) is so pestred with woods,
hedges, and bushes, that it is not only not habitable, but also trauailers ca^
scarcely find any way or passage thereunto, in such sort as I am persuaded
that there is no one place in all these low Count eis which has endured so
many miseries calamities and aduersi^ies as this place has. And
notwithstanding that the people are simple and still hold the fashion of the
(...) Brabanders, yet have they always continued in the obseruances of the
Catholic faith, so as even during the sway of here^ik^, here was not so much
as one found amongst them that changed his faith, whereby may be seen the
great mercy and love of God toward these miserable and afflicted people, the
which being brought under so many calamities he vouchsafes to comfort now
again by demonstration of his wounderful woorks amongst them.

THE FIFTH CHAPTER.
WHat should be the cause why our Lord vouchsafes to woork miracles in such a
part of the Country of Braban^, we may imagine (upon apparent reasons) that
he does it for two causes, upon apparent reasons I say we may imagine ( for
who is he that knows the mind of our Lord, or who is he that has been his
coun^^al^^?) first it has b^ne the custom of his divine providence when he
chastises any people for their correction and not for their destruction and
utter ruin, to ^end them at times some comfort by miraculous works of his
power and mercy. The people of Israel was afflicted by the servitude of Egypt,
by the stinging of serpents, by the invasion of the Madianits, and by other
like adue^sities, but to the end they might think that God had not altogether
forsaken them, he comforted them by transporting them over the red sea, he
cured them by the beholding of the brazen serpent, by staying of the Sun
under Iosue, by covering the fleece with dew lying on a dry place in the time
of Ge^eon, and by many other miracles. The Country of Italy was afflicted by
a cruel war betwixt the Gu^lphes and the Gibbelines, but our Lord vouchsafing
to show that he would send them a speedy deliverance translated thither
miraculouslie the hows of our Lady of Lau^etto.

Even so we verily hope that by these favors and miracles, the divine
bounty of God has given us to understand that he being moved with pity and
compassion upon these poor low Countries, will take away very soon the scourge
of his ire and indignation, to the end that we should not think our selves
wholly by him abandoned through the throng of so many oppressions and miseries,
and therefore he sends us now these tokens and presages of some deliverance
near at hand.

The other reason may be that those holy Saints which have dwelt and been borne
in this Country, and specially in Brabant have by their merits and prayers
obtained this grace of God that this place which they whiles they lived had in
their possession as their inheritance and patrimony wherein they were wont to
serve his divine majesty, or unto which they used to resort, being delivered
from the fury of heretics should be one day honored with so great miracles.

For this territory was once the patrimony of the first Saints of Brabant. It
is Molan. Not. SS. ^^lg.
well known that S. Bauo had in his possession the greatest part of the
Hasbaine of Brabant. It is also certain that S. Pipin Ian. 4. Feb. 21. Mar.
17. Iul* 10. and 16. Octo. 1. and 2^. Noue^b. 23.
first duke of Bra^ant had his abode in the town of Landen whereupon it bears
the surname: in the same place and with him lived his wife Iduberga, from
whence proceeded that holy offspring Gertrude and Begga, and of this Begga the
great Charlemaine with his issue is descended. In this said quarter also
dwelled being of the blood of the foresaid Pipin S. Amil^erga Dece^b. ^7.
mother of the holy virgins Raynildis, Pharaild^s, and Gudula, and long before
all these, S. Ermelinde a virgin right famous for her sanctity. Likewise S.
^rudo in his days possessed many places in the Has^aine and in the Campignie,
where he had preached the holy gospel, His mother Ad^la renowned for
miracles rests in a place called zel^^m near to the town of Die, which
place heretofore apperteined to her, and is but two miles distant Molan. lib de
sac. milit Duc. Braban.
from Montaigue. Add herunto that our Lord has remembrance of the zeal and
magnanimi^tie of the old dukes of Brabant, who a thousand years ago did so
valiantly maintain and advance the Christian Catholic faith against the
infidels Pagans, and heretics. And that it seems that the Country of
B^abant shalere long be who^e restored and settled again in the Catholic
faith, and in that glorious state wherein once it flourished.

THE SIXTH CHAPTER.
THat these things are done upon a Mountain is not without reao^s, in regard
that man being borne into this world to retire his mind from earth^^e to
heavenly things, our Lord, both in the old and new testament was always
wont to do his most special and principal works upon Mountains. Upon the
Mount Moria Isaac was saved, and Abraham received the benediction. Upon the
mount Sinay the law was delivered unto Moses. Upon the mount Horeb Helias saw
God almighty so much as a mortal man was ever able to see him, upon a Mountain
our Lord was wont to preach, upon the Mount Thab^r he was trasfigured, upon the
Mount Olives he prayed and upon the mount Calua^^ he was crucified, and
accomplished our salvation. Moreover the most famous places of the world where
the holy Mother of God is honored, are wyld Mountains and fu of wood, very
much resembling this our Mountain. They which have written the hutorie of
La^^e to, affirm that the little hovvs wherein the incarnation of our Lord was
first wrought and which was had in honor in the town of Nazareth, until the
year of our Lord 1291. (because Siria was conquered by the Sarracen^) was
taken from that place and transported into Europe into the Country of
Dalma^ia, and there set upon a little Mountain or hill nere to the ^owne of
T^rsactum. And for so much as ^n that place they did it not that honor which was
convenient, it came so to pass that 4. years and 7. months after, the said
little hovvs was by Angels transported into the marca of A^cona in Italy* and
set upon a Mountain or hill in a wood hard by the City of Recana^o, and a
li^le while after that, because the pilgrims were hindered to go freely thither
(through the multitude of Thieves and Robbers which made the passage dangerous)
it was miraculous^^e set upon an other hill belonging to two breethern, who
being fallen at variance for the offerings which were there made and our Lord
being offended with their controversies and covetousness, made the said little
house to be placed upon an other little Mountaigne not far from thence in the
year of our Lord 1294. where our good God woorketh (even to this present day)
continually many miracles and graces, to the comfort and strengthening of all
good Christians. What place is there in Spain more honorable and more renowned
for miracles, then that Mountain in the kingdom of Cath^lonia not far from
Barcelona called Mount-serrato? because it seems to have been cut with a saw,
and fitted for the service of the blessed virgin Marie, The which Mountain
(according to that which many credible persons have written) was in time past
in accessible through the wyndinges and troublesome passages which it has had:
and not habitable for the rocks and craggie stones which it contained, but at
this present, it is seemlie set forth with 13. hermitages and a goodly great
Monastery of S. Benedicts order, with many fair buildings, and a merueilous
multitude of Pilgrims. In which place since the year 801. until the year.
1599. there has been wrought 381. miracles, whereof a great part are approved
and confirmed by public and authentical testifications.

THE SEVENTH CHAPTER.
NEither seems it to want reason that our Lord would have his Mother to be
honored by her Image placed in an Oak, for the holy scriptures inform us that
our Lord God has wrought many miracles under diverse Oaks. For under the Oak of
Mambre according to the Hebrew text he concluded his league with Abraham, and
there promised him a son by his wife Sara. In the same place the Angels
appeared in form of me^, diverse times renewing in the name of God the foresaid
promise, and foretelling the destruction of the inhabitants of Sodom. under an
Oak nere unto Sic^e^ Jacob buried the Idols. Under the same Oak Ios^e renewed
the aliance between God and the people of Israel, as before we have declared.
Under the Oak in Ephra the angel ap ^ud 16.
peared to Ged^on whom he appointed Captain and leader of the people of
Israel, promising to free the^ out of the servitude, of the Madianits. Yea our
Lord Jesus Christ wrought our salvation upon the wood, of the cross which (as
Doctors affirm) was framed of Oak. The Pagans were wont to call the Oak, great
and holy, and dedicated it unto ^upiter their chief God, because in the
beginning of the world men made their food of acotnes, which is the fruit
thereof and the Romans had a custom to give him a crown of Oken leaves that
in war had saved the life of a Citizen of R^me. Of Oaks the Poet writes,
prophesying of the golden world out of the verses of the Si^illes saying.

The t^^e will come of peace and plenty so,

That from the Oak the honi^ sweet shall flow.

When therefore we consider the foresaid examples of the old testament why
should we not hope that nere unto this oak by Sic^en through the prayers of
holy and deuowt people. God may be inclined to make a new alliance and
reconciliatio^ betvv^xt himself and these lovv countries, that he vvil send the
Angel of vvisdome amongst us, bring the Idols of heresy and impiety to
nothing, raise us up some valerous Gedeons, grant us victory over these
Madianites, send comfort to the loyal subjects, strengthen both our souls and
bodies, and power down upon us the dew of his heavenly graces.

THE EIGHT CHAPTER.
THat our Lord has chose^ this place so nere unto our enemies which is scarce
six or seven hundredth paces distant from the river of Demer, beyond which
river all the Country is subject to the contributions robberies and exactions
of the enemies, so as upon a clear day one may see six or seven miles into the
country held by the heretics and rebels, we may thereby thinck that the
wisdom of God has ordained it, that not only the Catholics might have
occasion to exercise their p^ety, and seek for mercy at the hands of God but
also that he might bring back again those which are in error into the bosom
of the Catholic Church, by the means of these miracles: for that so great
miracles and such abundance should be wrought in a place so nere the heretics
noses, what other thing may we judge it, the^ a plain demonstration that the
hon^or do^ne to images, invocation of Saints, and pilgrimages made to holy
places, are things very p^ous and right acceptable to the majesty of God:
notwithstanding whatsoever blasphemies and scoffings, of all the rablement of
heretics to the co^t^ary.

Blessed be therefore the father of mercy and God of comfort, which comfort
us after this manner in all our tribulatio^s, who in the doleful estate of
these Countries, by the wonderful woorks of his mercy yields joy and
consolation to the godly, confirms those that either stagger or doubt in
their faith, and confounds the malice of heretics (God grant it may be to
their salvation) with a light of such unspeakable brightness. Blessed be also
the Mother of mercy, which procures such favors for us poor wretches. God
grant that her goodness will so dispose of us, that as at the arrival of her
hows from Nazareth to Ital^* that Country (which wa^ the^ oppressed with a
long and cruel war) gained peace and concord by her prayers to our Lord, so
likewise by this new devotion o^ Catholics at Mountague, the blessed virgin and
mother of God being moved to compassion, by the prayers tears and devout
sighs of good people, may obtein at the hands of God, for these low Countries
scourged with so long a civil war, and welnigh brought to utter ruin, that
the Idols of our sins may be buried under this Oak: that the heretyks may be
reduced to the union of the holy Catholyke, Apostolyke, and Roman Church, and
our rebels to their due obedience of their natural princes, that we may all
of us with one mouth, and in the same Catholic faith, under one Prince in
earth praise and honor our God, three persons in Trinity and one in unity,
and the blessed virgin Mary mother of our Lord Jesus Christ.

THE NINTH CHAPTER.
B^t to come to our history. In Braba^t there is a place full of hil^e^ and
woods and altogether uninhabited called in the Duitch tongue Scherp^n-^e^^^l, in
French Mountaigne. in English The sharp or rough hill, in regard of the
roughnes and barennes of the place, situated by the town of S^chen, and little
more then a quarter of a dutche mile distant from thence, and a good mile from
Die. Upon this place (being a hill somewhat higher then the rest of the
ground lying nere thereunto) grew an old Oak, which was somewhat great, whereunto
a certain Image of our lady was fastened, and there is a certain report and
old tradition amongst those people which live in that quarter, that a hundredth
years and more sithe^ce, there was a certain Shepherd who feeding his flock
along that Mountaigne fownd the fore said Image of our Lady lying on the
grownd, the which he determined to carry home with him, but the Image
miraculously became so heavy that he was neither able to carry it, nor to lift
it, yea himself became so immovable that he had no power to stir out of the
place, in such sort as he could not drive his f^ok homeward. The farmer his
Master much woundring at his Shepherds unaccustomed lingering, went himself to
seek him, And having understood the cause of his stay, perceived forthwith that
the Image which the Shepherd me^t to have carried home, was the^ame Image which
men used to honor at the Oak of Mountaigue. Wherefore he took it and without
any empeschment or resistance placed it again in the Oak. And the Shepherd as
though he had been unloosed and vn^ettered went his way home with his she^p
without any further difficulty. By the report of this miracle, the people that
neighboured thereabout being excited to devotion, began to yield reverence to
the place and Image, and repaired thither very often to crave help of the
blessed virgin, especialy against agues. The which things the most ancient
inhabitants of the place have auowched under their solemn oaths, that they
have hard their parents and ance^ors report: to wit, Arnould of Ecynde being
fourscore years of age, John S^innen being of 70. John M^mbors of 60. and
Allard of B^gard also of 60. years of age, all being inhabitants and
Escheuins of Sichen. But forasmuch as we have this only by tradition of
certain old people without any further authentical proof (although well
deserving credit heerin) we mean not so to affirm their reports as
altogether assured thereof. But will proceed to such other things, whereof we can
aleage so authentical and assured proofs, as none can justly doubt thereof.

THE TENTH CHAPTER.
IT is certain and well known that the foresaid Image remained in the Oak
until the year 1580, but shortly after and till the year 1587. was no Image
at all in that place, as we find by ^osse v^ Artric who yet lives has
affirmed under his o^he that he was healed of a long ^uartain agu in the year
^583.
information of sundry persons who in that time (to be cured of their agues)
although with evident danger visited that place, many being restored to health
notwithstanding (as is aforesaid) no Image was then there, whereby it may
appear that not only the Image, but also the very place is dedicated to the
service of God, and to the honor of his blessed Mother. How this first Image
was lost we yet know no certainty, but it is coniectured that it was destroyed
by heretics who from the year 1578 until the year 1586. ranged daily in
those parts pilling and spoiling the Country.

Afterward in the year 1587. a good old man being a Burgess and Escheum of
S^chen (of humility not willing to be named) considering the great concour^e
and devotion which people had to this place, and knowing that the first Image
was lost, went unto a devout and godly widow dwelling in Die, called Agnes
Fredericks having the custody of the Church of All^all was in the suburbs of
the said town of Die who had in her hows an Image of our lady made of wood,
placed in a little frame or tabernacle, which upon ernest entreaty she giving
unto him, he carried to Mountague, and caused it to be set up in the foresaid
Oak, in the place of the other which was lost; where it continued until lent
in the year 1602. at which time Sir Godsrey of Thie^^r^ncle Pastor of the
Church of S. Eustace in Su^e^, took the said Image out of the Oak, and set it
in a little Chapel made of wood of six foot long and five broad, hard by the
sa^d Oak. And the same he did in regard of the great devotion which he saw the
people had that came thither to be cured of a vehement headache then reigning
in those parts whereof very many found amendment. In which little Chapel the
said Image remained until such time as it was placed in the new Chapel which
now is built of stone.

THE ELEVENTH CHAPTER.
LEt us now therefore speak of the miraculous and liberal cures which our lord
has vouchsafed to woork upon those that have honored his blessed mother in
this place.

From time out of mind the people and inhabitants of Sichen and places
therabout, were wont to find singular help by our Lady at Mountague so often as
they found themselves attainted of any agues.

Of which thing Sir Godsrey of Thienwincle Curat of S. Eustace in Sichen, does
sufficiently testify: who reme^breth that (fifty years past) he being then
about six years old, was carried by his parents to Mou^tague, where he was
cured of an ague which he had at that time,

Also at such time as the Spanish soldiers were in mutiny in the town of
Die and that their Captains remained in the town of Sichen attending whiles
the said soldiers were paid, there was a certain Spanish Captain lodged in a
Burgeise hows who was sick of an ague for the space of a whole year, and by
reason of his sickness was so wayward and troublesome as none could content him.
It came to pass that the good wife of the hows told him that if he desired to
be freed of his ague, it were good he should visit our lady at Mountague,
seeing so many were there cu^ed: whereupon moved partly by her and partly by
persu^sion of his own servants, he went thither, and accomplishing there his
devotion, was there cured, and wholly delivered of his ague, behauing himself
afterward so modestly and peaceably as he seemed to have altered his nature.

Moreover as the whole town of Sichen can testify, there is scarce therein
one household whereof some one or other has not received help at Mountague. The
Curate and escheuins of Sichen affirm assuredly that in the year 1598 at what
time the Irish of the regiment of Sir Wiliima Stanley Coronel, were lodged
there, many of the^ were wont to use no other phisik or remedy for their
diseases, but to make their prayers at the foresaid place of Mountague, amongst
whom very many were healed, in such forte that Father Walter Talbo^ an Irish
priest, one of the society of Jesus (who at that time was their preachet and
ghostly father) was wont oftentimes to say with great admiration that this
place was in very singular manner chosen by God to advance there his Mothers
honor, for which cause he was moved to go thither sometymesdenoutly in
procession, acco^pained with the said Irish and the townsmen of Sichen. Whereof
he wrote to Father Thomas Salius, who was the Superior of the fathers of the
society, which attended upon the Catholic kings army in the low Countries.
Many also affirm that the like cures have happened to diverse soldiers of other
nations, which came thither in pilgrimage both of the garrison of Die and
Sichen. And some are of opinion, that the brute and fame of Mou^tague was
principally diuulged by the same soldiers, in other Cities and places unto
which they repaired.

THE TWELFTH CHAPTER.
THe which brute by Gods disposition, in short time grew to be so great, that
it moved an ^^^^^t number of people which have flocked thither upon devotion
from all parts, whereof many have been helped and conforted, as may appear by
so many crutches, staves, bands and other things (which the sick persons used
in their maladies) left and hanged about the Chapel. For in the beginning of
the month of October in the year 1603. there were reckned up, to the number of
135. Crutches and legs of wood, of such as were lame, and they were brought
thither within the space of 4. or 5. months, without n^mbring the bands and
trusles of such as were broken, and the shoes which were vnderlayd belonging
to such as limped, vvherof the number would have been at this present double,
if (through the negligence of those who served the place) they had not been
carried away by other pilgrims and passengers. All which things were
undoubtedly left there by them who received either cure or notable eas^ of
their maladies. And to the end we may speak a word touching the number of
pilgrims, it is known by the relation of diverse credible persons, how that
upon the eight of September in the foresaid year 1603. being the feast of the
Nat^^t^ie of our Lady, there were about twenty thousand pilgrims, and upon
other days there have come thither many thowsands, and daily yet comes
thither a great number from all Countries and quarters, and they not only of
the common forte, but also diverse Princes, Earls, Lords, Gentlemen and
Ladies, the rehersall of whose names would here be too tedious.

THE XIII. CHAPTER.
THe same is also confirmed by the offerings, in Silver, Coin, Candles, Images
of silver, and of other matter representing the limbs and persons of those who
there have either found cure or ease. The precious beads and other gifts in
silver, and very rich vestures for the Image of our lady, whereof the number is
very great. And it will not be from the purpose to specify and name some of
the^: First the Magistrates of the City of Bruxelles (wherein at that time the
plague was very hot) sent in the month of September 1603. to the honor of
our lady of Mountague a fair crown of silver guilt, with this inscription in
latin: Reginae Calorum SS. Dei Matri B. Virgini Mariae, Bruxella morb^ afflicta
supplex ^btu^^t. Anno 1603. Which is as much to say. The City of Bruxelles
being afflicted with the pestilent disease, in humble manner ^ffreth this
Crown to the Queen of heaven, to the most holy Mother of God, the blessed
virgin Mariae: in the year 1603. And very many of good credit did note, that
after that time the contagious disease that had raig ned all that summer began
to decrease and by little and little to be aswaged.

The Magistrates and people of A^t^erp (who hau^e of old been much obliged and
affectioned to our lady their patroness) made a present unto her at Mountague
the eight of December 1603. being the feast of her conception, of two silver
candlesticks very cunningly wrought with these verses and inscription.

Suscipe Sancta Pare^s ex v^t^ Ant^erpi^ manus: Quod dat, ne pe^tis crescat
ac^rb^ ^ues. I must not forget the most honorable and devout Princess Dame
D^r^the of L^raine, widow to the Duke of Brunswick, who upon the eight of
October in the year aforesaid 1603. offered at the said place unto our Lady, a
fair ornament to hang before the Altar, recommending unto our blessed Lady her
^ourney, which she took out of these low Countries into Loraine.

The noble lady Dame Elcon^r Henriq ez de G^zman, widow to the Earl of Vseda,
Chief Cha^berlaine of the most gracious Infant of Spayn, the lady Isabella
Duchess of Brabant, presented there a fair gilt cup.

Omitting to make mention of a goodly foundation made to the Chapel of our
Lady, by a principal person of the court, who desired his name should be
concealed: and of many other gifts and offerings made there by diverse others,
whose reward is like to be great before God.

THE FOVRTENTH CHAPTER.
Our most gracious and devout Princes Albertus and Isabella are not to be
omitted: for first the Archduke Albertus returning from raising the siege of
Botsleduke, which was beleagred by the rebels of Holland, and going to
Bruxelles came to Mountague, on the tenth of November 1603, where he gave
thanks to our lord for his victory, and there did honor to his holy Moother,
and some few days after, he went thither another time from thesa^d City of
Bruxelles, together with the Duchess his wife, and all his Court, and very
many lords and gentlemen: where beein^ arrived upon the twentieth of the-said
month of November, the said Archduke and Duchess, as right devout Princes,
with all the lords and ladies of their trayn, made their prayers upon their
knees for a long space, as well for their own as for their countries welfare:
and from thence they went to lodge in the town of Dust, which is distant a
duitche mile and a half from that place. And after they had made their
preparation by fasting and confession, to receive the sacred communion,
returned the next day (which was the feast of the presentation of our Lady) to
4 english mile and a half.
Mountague on foot, and all their trayn, an^ there devoutly hard three masses,
at the first whereof the Princes only did communicate, at the second all the
ladies (...) estate and gentlewomen of honor to the An ex^ple of a deuo^t
court.
Princess, and at the third all the rest of the Court. And to the end their

own hands* And besides this, the most gracious Archduk Albertus caused a notable
alms to be given over and besides that which he had offered at his return from

where they surceased not to have care of the good ordering and adorning of the
